These hosts are designed with electron-deficient aromatic surfaces (such as perfluorinated arenes, naphthalenediimides, or other π-acidic systems) that can stabilize anions via noncovalent anion-π interactions. The most well-known types include:\n\n1. Aromatic macrocycles: Macrocyclic hosts incorporating electron-deficient aromatic rings (e.g., perfluorinated cyclophanes, naphthalenediimide-based macrocycles) can encapsulate anions within their cavities, positioning the anion above or between π-acidic surfaces for strong anion-π binding.\n\n2. Acyclic π-acidic receptors: Linear or branched molecules with multiple π-acidic aromatic units arranged to converge on an anion, maximizing anion-π contact.\n\n3. Molecular cages and capsules: 3D hosts with internal π-acidic surfaces (e.g., cages built from naphthalenediimide or perfluoroarene panels) that can encapsulate anions and stabilize them through multiple anion-π interactions.\n\nKey examples include:\n- Perfluorinated arene-based hosts (e.g., perfluorobenzene cyclophanes)\n- Naphthalenediimide (NDI) macrocycles and cages\n- Triazine-based π-acidic hosts\n\nThese systems are distinct from traditional anion receptors that rely on hydrogen bonding or electrostatic interactions. The design and function of these hosts are often discussed in the context of supramolecular chemistry, anion recognition, and noncovalent π interactions.\n\nReferences: Chem. The most well-known types include:\n\n1.
